  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

  <span id="freeText3396742712328047901" style="display:none">The master of a Victorian mansion dies suddenly – and his sister is convinced it was murder…. When Cora is savagely murdered with a hatchet, the extraordinary remark she made the previous day at her brother Richard’s funeral suddenly takes on a chilling significance. At the reading of Richard’s will, Cora was clearly heard to say: ‘It’s been hushed up very nicely, hasn’t it…But he was murdered, wasn’t he?’ In desperation, the family solicitor turns to Hercule Poirot to unravel the mystery.</span>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/174943-is-george-the-son-of-richard-wiki-page">Is George the son of Richard?

wiki page (http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/After_the_Funeral) says

&quot;George Crossfield, supposedly the nephew to Richard Abernethie but actually the son, solicitor in a stock broker&#39;s office, and an Abernethie heir&quot;

I&#39;m a bit lost -- I only read the book&#39;s Chinese version, but got no impression that George is Richard&#39;s son. Is it I missed something? or the translation is incomplete?</a>
</div>

<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/58079842-miguel-ros">Miguel Ros</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">In the book, George is Richard's nephew and that's all. He becomes the son of Helen in the TV movie with David Suchet. And then it turns out, in the T<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">In the book, George is Richard's nephew and that's all. He becomes the son of Helen in the TV movie with David Suchet. And then it turns out, in the TV movie I mean, he's Richard's son with Helen, instead of Leo's son with Helen.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1181660776698372893">This isn't one of my girl's best because it is "Poirot light". He only appears a handful of times but it is still fabulous because the family dynamics are Cuckoo for Cocoa Puffs and I am always in for that.<br/><br/>I also loved that Poirot was upstaged in solving this one and as usual I was blown away by both the who and the why.<br/><br/>Rock on Aggie.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1615777083" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1615777083" />
    <a title="Sumit RK" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38271102-sumit-rk"><img alt="Sumit RK" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599896274p2/38271102.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1615777083?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sumit RK" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sumit RK" href="/user/show/38271102-sumit-rk">Sumit RK</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1615777083" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2073948126644732627"><i>For in the long run, either through a lie, or through truth, people were bound to give themselves away…</i><br/><p>A Poirot mystery set in the post World War II Era. A sad reminder that Poirot is now old & retired & that the writer has now slowly started moving away from the Poirot character… An era where Christie had started focusing more on Marple than Poirot.</p> <br/><p><b><u>Story: </u></b> A wealthy man dies & his relatives gather after his funeral for the reading of his will, during which his sister states that he was murdere</p></span>
  <span id="freeText2073948126644732627" style="display:none"><i>For in the long run, either through a lie, or through truth, people were bound to give themselves away…</i><br /><p>A Poirot mystery set in the post World War II Era. A sad reminder that Poirot is now old &amp; retired &amp; that the writer has now slowly started moving away from the Poirot character… An era where Christie had started focusing more on Marple than Poirot.</p> <br /><p><b><u>Story: </u></b> A wealthy man dies &amp; his relatives gather after his funeral for the reading of his will, during which his sister states that he was murdered. The next day, she herself is found murdered. Poirot is called in to solve the mystery.<br />Without revealing the plot too much, I will get into what works &amp; what doesn’t work in terms of the storyline:</p><br /><p><b><u>What Works</u></b>: After the Funeral has the ideal setting for a perfect murder mystery; a dead rich man, plenty of greedy relatives &amp; reading of a dead man’s will. Christie is brilliant at murder mysteries involving multiple scheming &amp; backstabbing suspects ( <i>And Then There Were None &amp; Murder On The Orient Express </i> come to mind), where every member is a suspect and enough clues are left around to suspect every one of the murder. Christie keeps confusing the readers with clues while blinding them to the obvious facts right in front of them. Indeed, hidden amongst the plot are many of Christie’s usual tricks &amp; hints which guide &amp; sometimes misguide you.</p> <br /><p><b><u>What Doesn’t Work</u></b>: There are a lot of characters at the start which you have to get your head around. The chapters are split into parts each dedicated to a different character with alibis of each character deconstructed. The many characters introduced all at once &amp; their stories, all gets confusing &amp; it slightly distracts you from the main mystery. Although this is a Poirot novel, for about the first two-thirds of the novel the investigation is conducted by Entwhistle, the family solicitor. Poirot is brought in at the end– almost as an afterthought for the final reveal. (as discussed above) Poirot’s solution to the murder mystery is quite logical and satisfying, though it does have a few loopholes, if you think about it.</p><br /><p>Overall, though not among Christie’s best works, it is still a very good &amp; enjoyable mystery with a unique twist in the end. 3.5/5</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="2073948126644732627" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1266132903" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1266132903" />
    <a title="carol. " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3978225-carol"><img alt="carol. " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307934801p2/3978225.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1266132903?book_show_action=true">Apr 27, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="carol. " class="user" itemprop="url" name="carol. " href="/user/show/3978225-carol">carol. </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781579127312" href="/book/show/1359973.After_the_Funeral">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fans of Christie, spare writing, Poirot</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3978225-carol?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1266132903" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9192703132047545985">from my blog, where it is much easier to give links, pictures and half-stars at <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://clsiewert.wordpress.com/2015/04/30/after-the-funeral-by-agatha-christie/'>https://clsiewert.wordpress.com/2015/...</a><br/><br/>Goodness, but I’m a reading disaster when it comes to Christie books. At one point in <i>After the Funeral,</i> I felt I knew who the murderer was, and when I flipped to check if I was right (<i>oh, the horror!)</i>–yes, I did that—I <i>was.</i> But I got no pleasure out of my powers of deduction, as I’m almost positive I’ve read this at least once before. Possibly <i>twice.</i> So that’s a sad statement </span>
  <span id="freeText9192703132047545985" style="display:none">from my blog, where it is much easier to give links, pictures and half-stars at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://clsiewert.wordpress.com/2015/04/30/after-the-funeral-by-agatha-christie/">https://clsiewert.wordpress.com/2015/...</a><br><br>Goodness, but I’m a reading disaster when it comes to Christie books. At one point in <i>After the Funeral,</i> I felt I knew who the murderer was, and when I flipped to check if I was right (<i>oh, the horror!)</i>–yes, I did that—I <i>was.</i> But I got no pleasure out of my powers of deduction, as I’m almost positive I’ve read this at least once before. Possibly <i>twice.</i> So that’s a sad statement of my mental affairs that I’m almost pleased by solving the murderer of a book I’ve read twice before. Sigh–if it doesn’t pertain to biology, it likely doesn’t stick in my brain. So I find I’m unable to advise if it was a ‘fair’ or ‘solvable’ mystery, for those who look for that sort of thing. I rather think it wasn’t. But at any rate, murderer identified, I was able to settle down and concentrate on Christie’s fine storytelling. That Dame sure can tell a tale, because it remained no less suspenseful.<br><br>I’m working on a theory that Christie was a master mystery writer. Oh, I know; the British Empire already figured that out in 1971. But really, the woman could write. I am so amazed, sometimes, how she created so much character in a handful of words. I know I’ve said this before, but it’s something that bears examining. Why is it that Rothfuss and Sanderson get heaps of accolades when they describe every single jewel someone is wearing, taking 700 pages to tell their story about a journey of a thousand steps? I think–and now that I spell this out, I think there’s something really quite valid to my instinct here–that I prefer the character of a story, the sense of it. I don’t need the high-def, cinematic version–I want the emotion of it, the presence of it. Max Gladstone recently wrote a fascinating post about action scenes (<a href="http://www.maxgladstone.com/2015/04/fighting-words/" rel="nofollow noopener">“Fighting Words”</a>), and at the very bottom, in the comments section, Kameron Hurley comments: “Yup, this is how I think about it: it’s not my job to give the literal then this, then that, then this, but to infer enough of the scene through the emotion I convey for the reader to *fill in the gaps.* ”<br><br>^This.<br><br><br>If I may move from the discussion of writing action to the concept of writing, period, Christie doesn’t (exhaustively) describe how each person walks, the sound of their voice, their dress, their mannerisms; she picks out the part that identifies them most, includes that description in an action, and lets the reader draw the conclusion. For me, to mix my metaphors again, it’s the difference between 17th century Dutch paintings and cubism, particularly Braque, one of my favorite painters (although not this one):<br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1430456263i/14714773._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>versus<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1430456263i/14714774.jpg" width='360"' height="" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>I think that’s why Christie works for me. There’s a combination of specificity and ambiguity that gives an impression, with out the need to delineate every shadow. She allows my own interpretation, and yet every single time, I end up exactly where she wants me. More or less.<br><br>In <i>After the Funeral,</i> everyone gathers at the estate for the funeral of Richard Abernethie, and imagine the surprise among the clan when dotty, arty Aunt Cora says, <b>“But he was murdered, wasn’t he?”</b> Elderly solicitor Entwistle remains bothered, her remark nagging at him, and imagine his surprise when he receives a phone call the next day from the police. I won’t spoil any more, but Christie does trot in her favorites: the ancient family butler, the motherly wife, the gambler, the hypochondriac, the actress, the scatty matron, the stockbroker of questionable values. And, of course, the Monsieur himself:<br><b><br>“‘Hercule Poirot–at your service.’<br>Poirot bowed.<br>There were no gasps of astonishment or of apprehension.”<br></b><br>And such a snicker we all had at Poirot’s expense, did we not? And with virtually no set-up, we laughed. Now try this brief character appearance on for size:<br><b><br>” Mr. Entwistle passed a very restless night. He felt so tired and so unwell in the morning that he did not get up.<br> His sister who kept house for him brought up his breakfast on a tray and explained to him severely how wrong he had been to go gadding off to the North of England at his age and in his frail state of health.<br> Mr. Entwistle contented himself with saying that Richard Abernethie had been a very old friend.<br> ‘Funerals!’ said his sister with deep disapproval. ‘Funerals are absolutely fatal for a man your age!”<br></b><br>In four very brief paragraphs, we have the entire sense of Mr. Entwistle’s sister, do we not? And their decades of interaction? And had another little snicker at his sister’s comment? Even more surprising: there were three more paragraphs to follow, all on a chapter heading page. Eat your heart out, <i>Way of Kings!</i><br><br><br>This book? I recommend it, for fans of both Christie and Poirot. It feels a little routine for her at this point, but it is a well-polished routine, with a nice twist. Even more, I recommend Dame Christie. Period.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9192703132047545985" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer63137965" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14202008163787843532">Funerals Are Fatal = After the Funeral (Hercule Poirot #31), Agatha Christie<br/><br/>After the Funeral is a work of detective fiction by Agatha Christie and first published in March 1953.<br/><br/>The book features the author's Belgian detective Hercule Poirot, but the Murder at the Gallop film adaptation instead featured her amateur sleuth, Miss Marple. <br/><br/>A wealthy man dies at home. His relatives gather after his funeral for the reading of his will, during which his sister states that he was murdered. The next day,</span>
  <span id="freeText14202008163787843532" style="display:none">Funerals Are Fatal = After the Funeral (Hercule Poirot #31), Agatha Christie<br /><br />After the Funeral is a work of detective fiction by Agatha Christie and first published in March 1953.<br /><br />The book features the author's Belgian detective Hercule Poirot, but the Murder at the Gallop film adaptation instead featured her amateur sleuth, Miss Marple. <br /><br />A wealthy man dies at home. His relatives gather after his funeral for the reading of his will, during which his sister states that he was murdered. The next day, she herself is found murdered. Poirot is called in to solve the mystery.<br /><br />عنوانها: بعد از تشییع جنازه؛ پس از تشییع جنازه؛ نویسنده: آگاتا (میلر) کریستی؛ تاریخ نخستین خوانش: سال 2001 میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: بعد از تشییع جنازه؛ نویسنده: آگاتا (میلر) کریستی؛ مترجم رویا سعیدی؛ تهران، کتابهای هرمس؛ 1379؛ در 317ص؛ فروست داستانهای آگاتا کریستی: سری هرکول پوارو شماره سی و یک؛ چاپ دوم 1384؛ سوم 1387، شابک 9789647100632؛ موضوع داستانهای کارآگاهی از نویسندگان انگلیسی - سده 20م<br /><br />عنوان: پس از تشییع جنازه؛ نویسنده: آگاتا (میلر) کریستی؛ مترجم رویا سعیدی؛ تهران، کتابهای هرمس؛ چاپ سوم 1387؛ در 317ص؛ فروست داستانهای آگاتا کریستی: سری هرکول پوارو سی و یک؛ شابک 9789647100632؛ چاپ چهارم 1390؛<br /><br />نقل از متن: «پوآرو با دقت ویژه ای افراد را یک به یک از زیر نگاه خود می‌گذراند؛ نخست نگاهش را به سوزان دوخت، که صاف نشسته بود و بشاش و سرحال به ‌نظر می‌رسید؛ سپس نوبت به شوهر «سوزان» رسید، که نزدیک «سوزان» نشسته بود، با چهره ‌ای بی‌حالت، و داشت یک سیم را به دور انگشتانش حلقه می‌کرد.؛ نگاه «پوآرو» از روی «گرِگوری» به روی «جرج کراسفیلد» لغزید؛ او داشت درباره حقه ‌بازیهای قماربازان، با «رزاموند» صحبت می‌کرد، و «رزاموند» با بی‌علاقگی و کاملاً بدون فکر می‌گفت: «آه چه جالب! اما چرا؟».؛ نفر بعد «مایکل» بود، با آن نگاههای خسته و در عین حال مطبوع و دلنشین؛ مردی با جذابیتی انکارناپذیر که در آن جمع همتایی نداشت.؛ سپس نگاه «پوآرو» به «هلن» خیره شد، که باوقار و اندکی دور از بقیه نشسته بود.؛ «تیموتی» نفر بعد بود؛ او در بهترین مبل لم داده بود، و یک بالش اضافی هم در زیر کمرش قرار داشت.؛ «مود» در کنار «تیموتی» بود، محکم و مصمم با هیکلی تنومند؛ و آماده خدمت به «تیموتی».؛ آخرین نفر «گیل‌کریست« بود که در خارج از جمع خانواده نشسته بود؛ بلوزی بسیار شیک بر تن داشت، و می‌شد در چهره ‌اش خواند، که از اینکه به آن جمع راه یافته سپاسگزار است.؛ «پوآرو» با خود گفت که بزودی «گیل‌کریست» برمی‌خیزد، و بهانه ‌ای می‌آورد، و این جمع خانوادگی را ترک می‌کند، و به اتاق خودش می‌رود.؛ «گیل‌کریست» مقام خودش را می‌دانست؛ و این را به طریقی دشوار آموخته بود»؛ پایان نقل<br /><br />نقل نمونه متن دیگر: (میخواستم اسم آن کافه تریا را «نخل زیتون» بگذارم، شترهای کوچکی درست کنم، و صورت غذا را در آنها جای دهم، گاهی، چینی خیلی قشنگی پیدا میشود، معمولا جزو کالاهای مرجوعی، نمیخواستم از آن ظروف سفیدرنگ مسخره، استفاده کنم.؛ کافه را در جایی بازمیکردم، که آدمهای درست و حسابی و با نزاکت، به آنجا بیایند.؛ سپس با خود زمزمه کرد: «میزهای چوب بلوط، صندلیهای حصیری، بالشهای راهراه قرمز و سفید») پایان نقل از کتاب<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 26/04/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="14202008163787843532" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer1424363322651791044">EXCERPT:"I may," said Poirot in a completely unconvinced tone, "be wrong."<br/>Morton smiled. "But that doesn't often happen to you?"<br/>"No. Though I will admit - yes, I am forced to admit - that it has happened to me."<br/>"I must say I'm glad to hear it! To be always right must be sometimes monotonous."<br/>"I do not find it so," Poirot assured him.<br/><br/>ABOUT THE BOOK: Hercule Poirot is called on to investigate the murder of a brother and sister in this classic from the Queen of Mystery.<br/><br/>When Cora Lansquenet is savag</span>
  <span id="freeText1424363322651791044" style="display:none">EXCERPT:"I may," said Poirot in a completely unconvinced tone, "be wrong."<br />Morton smiled. "But that doesn't often happen to you?"<br />"No. Though I will admit - yes, I am forced to admit - that it has happened to me."<br />"I must say I'm glad to hear it! To be always right must be sometimes monotonous."<br />"I do not find it so," Poirot assured him.<br /><br />ABOUT THE BOOK: Hercule Poirot is called on to investigate the murder of a brother and sister in this classic from the Queen of Mystery.<br /><br />When Cora Lansquenet is savagely murdered, the odd remark she made the day before at her brother's funeral becomes chillingly important: "It's been hushed up very nicely, hasn't it. . . . But he was murdered, wasn't he?"<br /><br />Desperate to learn more about both deaths, the family solicitor turns to detective extraordinaire Hercule Poirot to unravel the mystery. . . . <br /><br />MY THOUGHTS: I think that After the Funeral is one of Agatha Christie's best books. It is more of a mystery and less of a vehicle to showcase the talents of Hercule Poirot, although he does, of course, solve the crime in the end. It is a very clever, inventive and unpredictable story. And thoroughly enjoyable. Even upon repeat readings/hearings. <br /><br />I particularly enjoyed the BBC production, especially Frank Thornton's role as the family solicitor. He has long been a firm favorite of mine as Truly of the Yard in Last of the Summer Wine. <br /><br />4.5 shimmering stars for After the Funeral by Agatha Christie, narrated by John Moffat, Frank Thornton and Jill Balcon, produced by BBC Audiobooks. All opinions expressed in this review are entirely my own personal opinions. <br /><br />Please refer to my Goodreads.com profile page or the 'about' page on sandysbookaday.wordpress.com for an explanation of my rating system. <br /><br />This review and others are also published on my blog sandysbookaday.wordpress.com</span>

    <a title="Evgeny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9340136-evgeny"><img alt="Evgeny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544797852p2/9340136.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3350739836?book_show_action=true">May 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Evgeny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Evgeny" href="/user/show/9340136-evgeny">Evgeny</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9340136-evgeny?shelf=hercule-poirot">hercule-poirot</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9340136-evgeny?shelf=crime-and-mystery">crime-and-mystery</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3350739836" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17056355263283220673">A rich guy had decency to die just in time for his surviving relatives to inherit his money just when they needed it. It so happened that they all desperately needed it at that time. Right after the funeral during the reading of the will one of them suggested the dead guy was helped to the afterlife - quite tactlessly I might add. <br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1590625829i/29552431._SX540_.jpg' alt='Reading of the will' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>The family lawyer Mr. Entwistle thought it was peculiar. <br/><br/>A couple of days later the tactless relative was brutally murdered. Again Mr. Entwistle thought it was peculia</span>
  <span id="freeText17056355263283220673" style="display:none">A rich guy had decency to die just in time for his surviving relatives to inherit his money just when they needed it. It so happened that they all desperately needed it at that time. Right after the funeral during the reading of the will one of them suggested the dead guy was helped to the afterlife - quite tactlessly I might add. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1590625829i/29552431._SX540_.jpg" alt="Reading of the will" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>The family lawyer Mr. Entwistle thought it was peculiar. <br><br>A couple of days later the tactless relative was brutally murdered. Again Mr. Entwistle thought it was peculiar. After some other gruesome event (no spoilers) Mr. Entwistle thought it was so peculiar he decided to investigate. He knew all those people so asking them questions was not a problem. He was very much puzzled by his findings. <br><br>Did you notice something? Lots of things happened, but Hercule Poirot was not in the story yet. He briefly appeared in chapter 7 only to disappear again for a really long time yielding stage to lots of people with different kinds of family drama. I have nothing against reading well-written family drama, but I was expecting Poirot instead, dammit! <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1590625638i/29552419._SX540_.png" alt="Family drama" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Finally the great detective bothered to appear, just in time to explain the mystery. Usually this behavior was typical for miss Marple, but not Poirot. In any way, his observations, deductions, and explanations were brilliant, but at this point they came too little, too late. I also found the motive for such an elaborated crime to be bland and underwhelming. <br><br>All in all, as a family drama it is worth 5 stars. <br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1590625700i/29552428.jpg" alt="Another drama" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>As a Poirot mystery it is 3 stars. Taking an average gives 4 stars. I will leave it at that, but this is one of the weakest (if not <b>the</b> weakest) Poirot mystery I gave such a high rating. </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2065128117" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2065128117" />
    <a title="David Schaafsma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma"><img alt="David Schaafsma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574997804p2/269235.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2065128117?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Schaafsma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Schaafsma" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma">David Schaafsma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780553350166" href="/book/show/489902.Funerals_are_Fatal">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=mystery-detective-thriller">mystery-detective-thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=christie">christie</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2065128117" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11772526444311018008">Agatha Christie’s #31 Hercule Poirot mystery is the work of a writer that at this point knows her craft so well that she can do it almost in her sleep. And this story has a lot of sleep in it, and dreams. And a couple of funerals. I preferred the original title, After the Funeral, because it speaks to the more serious literary tone of this book (compared to the more comic tone she often seems to be going for, that would match Funerals are Fatal, which isn’t exactly accurate, as you are led to be</span>
  <span id="freeText11772526444311018008" style="display:none">Agatha Christie’s #31 Hercule Poirot mystery is the work of a writer that at this point knows her craft so well that she can do it almost in her sleep. And this story has a lot of sleep in it, and dreams. And a couple of funerals. I preferred the original title, After the Funeral, because it speaks to the more serious literary tone of this book (compared to the more comic tone she often seems to be going for, that would match Funerals are Fatal, which isn’t exactly accurate, as you are led to believe that a murder happens at a funeral, which it does not). The tone herein this book is not actually comic but serious, with a touch of madness.<br /><br />In almost all Christie books there are bystanders who suppose that the crime must have been done by a “madman,” and it almost never is true. Poirot is a psychological detective. He operates by getting to know the possible murderers and thinking—using his little grey cells—and proceeding, not through the available evidence, that’s police work, only technical—but through ratiocination, through step-by-step logic as it applies to the study of human nature. So both Poirot and I think Christie also don't feel that "madness" is a central rationale for murder. Or interesting, really. Madmen are random, and thus boringly illogical, not worth the trouble to Christie and Poirot. Oh, we do have our daffy characters here, one possibly quite mad, Timothy, and one pretending to be mad, Greg, but finally those types are always comic relief, distractions. Which is not say madness is irrelevant to this tale, but . . . you won’t get me to tell, I won’t!<br /><br />But back to the point: Poirot operates through logic, and talking to people, through what he calls “the dangers of conversation,” As he says, “if you can induce a person to talk to you for long enough, on any subject whatever, sooner or later they will give themselves away. [Name redacted] did.”<br /><br />One of the features of this tale that finally makes Christie impressive in this particular book and not just ploddingly proficient is that Christie increasingly reveals her love/hate relationship with her little round Belgian, whom she likes to make fun of for his arrogance. No one in this tale, he is disappointed to discover, even has heard of him. She—wickedly—as they all refer to him as “Mr. Pontarlier.” His name is his brand, his cache, and they get it wrong! Sacre bleu!<br /><br />And she makes fun of his way of speaking: <br /><br />Poirot: “The time has come to tell you all.”<br /><br />Inspector Morton: “You sound like a young lady in a Victorian melodrama.”<br /><br />And he does, in this tale of all things Victorian! And at this point, dozens of books done, Christie feels forced to write him in this way! Augh! But she both does what she has to do, she makes Poirot come alive, and then she simultaneously satirizes him. Can he actually be to the masses both adorable and annoying? Looks like it.<br /><br />And then Morton can’t resist saying what we as readers are thinking, something Christie perhaps might admit herself about her now close to fiftieth book, which she really does want to finish:<br /><br />“Yes, yes, tell me all! And for the Lord’s sake hurry up and do it!”<br /><br />Which he does, of course, finally. But here again she is making fun of Poirot for his necessarily meticulous (and sometimes admittedly boring, and long) method, which she is compelled to recreate, book after book, so tongue-in-cheek makes fun of herself for being trapped to write him in this way! <br /><br />But then the resolution, the ingenuity of which makes you realize that it is all necessary, and moreover (such a Victorian word!) worth it! So in the end, all is forgiven, we love Poirot after all, and so does Agatha! Which is why she is Dame Christie, and not Detective Nobody! <br /><br />(Spoilers in here, but not THE spoiler, I wouldn’t do that). The facts of the case: Richard Abernethie dies. After the funeral Cora Lasquenet says she thinks it was murder, and for which she is herself murdered. After which Miss Gilchrist, Cora’s companion, who may know the truth about Cora, is taken ill eating a piece of wedding cake laced with arsenic. And after that Mrs. Abernethie, who may know the truth about Cora, is conked on the head, concussed, hospitalized. Will she recover, to reveal the truth?! Oh, have it either way, because we have M. Poirot!<br /><br />But seriously, what do we get for speaking the truth at odd moments? Or are they lies? What do we really know about Cora? Or about anyone?<br /><br />You think as you read that details are just details, but Christie will make you pay attention, again and again, because some mundane details figure in to make this a sort of dazzling resolution, among them: <br /><br />“The visit of an art critic, a smell of oil paint, a picture postcard of Polflexan harbor and finally a bouquet of wax flowers standing on that malachite table. . .” <br /><br />And a telegram with these four words: "Definitely a Vermeer, Guthrie."<br /><br />And the imperfect reflection of people in mirrors. Poirot: “To see ourselves as others see us!” <br /><br />And Victorian stolidity and aesthetics and values and “ladylike” manners being replaced by something rather modern. <br /><br />And a consideration of the nature of madness.<br /><br />And, just randomly, I like this line, from Timothy: “I’ve a soul above corn plasters, Entwhistle.” I didn't know what this meant, no, but it made me smile. But one would hope we all would have souls above corn plasters, oui?<br /><br />Reading along in this book, do you have the temerity to be bored?! You must have patience, mon ami! A bit of advice about method, to which both the Inspector and Poirot ascribe:<br /><br />"You don't want to fluster your bird too soon. But when you do fluster it, you want to fluster it well." And Poirot does, finally. He patiently builds his case, and then flusters the bird well in a final explosion of flustering!<br /><br />I had never heard of Funerals are Fatal, and thought for 200 or so of the 224 pages that this was just another 3 starred book, oh, come on, get it over with, but the ending—gathering together all the relevant motifs—brought it to 4 stars. What did I know? What do I ever know? It’s maybe even better than that, really, compared to any other mysteries being written, but alas, I am getting a little tired of Christie in spite of myself. 30 books! Sacre bleu! Only 9 to go!</span>
  <a data-text-id="11772526444311018008" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5404968043466978871">A fabulous Poirot. More tomorrow<br/><br/>So I, and a number of other people are now well into the third year of our Group Buddy Read of all of Agatha Christie's Poirot Novels and short stories, and whilst some of the novels fade in my memory, I can imagine this one will stay with me.<br/><br/>Poirot is asked by an old acquaintance and family lawyer to investigate the possible murder of his old friend and also the murder of his old friends sister just days after the funeral. Is there even a connection especially hi</span>
  <span id="freeText5404968043466978871" style="display:none">A fabulous Poirot. More tomorrow<br /><br />So I, and a number of other people are now well into the third year of our Group Buddy Read of all of Agatha Christie's Poirot Novels and short stories, and whilst some of the novels fade in my memory, I can imagine this one will stay with me.<br /><br />Poirot is asked by an old acquaintance and family lawyer to investigate the possible murder of his old friend and also the murder of his old friends sister just days after the funeral. Is there even a connection especially his sister blurts out she thinks her brother was murdered. A number of nieces and nephews as well as the final brother and his wife are the immediate beneficiaries of the will and are therefore prime suspects.<br /><br />Poirot poses as the leader of a refugee charity interested in purchasing the deceased man's stately home to enable him to investigate the possible suspects, all of whom could be involved.<br /><br />A wonderful example of Poirot's little grey cells in action, and the epitome of what Christie set out to create with her fabulous detective.</span>

  <span id="freeText11596356012970124546" style="display:none">I couldn't let Dame Agatha's 130th Birthday pass without reading a Poirot - especially as it's also the hundredth anniversary of his first appearance in Christie's debut novel.<br /><br />It was a slight shame that this actually turned out to be a Poirot-lite mystery, though the family drama aspect of following the array of potential suspects actually make for a great whodunit with numerous red herrings.<br /><br />Following the funeral of Richard Abernethie his sister Cora declares that she thinks his death wasn't through natural causes, only to be brutally murdered the next day.<br /><br />Astue Christie readers will look for all the usual clues that is synonymous with her puzzle mystery's, there was a few occasions I thought I'd worked it out.<br />But yet again it's not until Poirot's summing up during the conclusion that everything finally clicked into place.<br /><br />Yet again the queen of crime had fooled me again and shows the longevity of these novels. </span>

<div id="review_1834515067" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1834515067" />
    <a title="Jason Koivu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu"><img alt="Jason Koivu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560521450p2/1742824.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1834515067?book_show_action=true">Nov 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason Koivu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason Koivu" href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu">Jason Koivu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=crime">crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=detective">detective</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1834515067" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12878401195475768232">I used to make fun of Scooby Doo and how the gang would unmask the villain at the end, specifically how the villain would inevitably and hurriedly admit to the crime. Then I started reading Agatha Christie's books and I realized where they picked up the habit.<br/><br/>Now, don't get me wrong, Christie's mysteries are wonderful reads. So much fun! I especially love Hercule Poirot. It's a shame it takes such a long time for him to show up in <i>After the Funeral.</i> Much of the groundwork is laid out by a lawyer</span>
  <span id="freeText12878401195475768232" style="display:none">I used to make fun of Scooby Doo and how the gang would unmask the villain at the end, specifically how the villain would inevitably and hurriedly admit to the crime. Then I started reading Agatha Christie's books and I realized where they picked up the habit.<br /><br />Now, don't get me wrong, Christie's mysteries are wonderful reads. So much fun! I especially love Hercule Poirot. It's a shame it takes such a long time for him to show up in <i>After the Funeral.</i> Much of the groundwork is laid out by a lawyer before Poirot arrives on the scene to tie it all up neatly.<br /><br />The body of this book is quite good. The murderer is nicely disguised. The red herrings are well-stocked. But then comes the end. It's a satisfactory end as far as solving a crime goes, however, here again the murderer blurts out the truth. Certainly Poirot has the person cornered and it would just be a matter of a trial to have the person convicted, but then that wouldn't be as dramatic, would it? No. It would be more realistic though, and that's the problem with such endings. They somewhat tarnish an otherwise fairly believable story. <br /><br />But that is a minor point as far as my enjoyment of the entire book goes. Yes, I have spent a good portion of a rather short review going on about it, but honestly, everything else about <i>After the Funeral</i> is a good read through and through!</span>
  <a data-text-id="12878401195475768232" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3302927971" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3302927971" />
    <a title="Piyangie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie"><img alt="Piyangie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590579939p2/48056505.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3302927971?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Piyangie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Piyangie" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie">Piyangie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/23123332-after-the-funeral">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=classic-mystery">classic-mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3302927971" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17702642761827008341">Agatha Christie never runs out on ideas, now, does she? And this is her 31st installment of the Poirot series, among others. <br/><br/><i> After the Funeral </i> is another great murder-mystery story by her. It is a very interesting story with a strong, intelligent, and original plot. I was quite taken in for a ride with so many turns that I was a little disoriented as to the crime and the criminal. I assure you, the crime was baffling too because after all, <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[ one turned out to be a hoax!  <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide sp</a></span></span>
  <span id="freeText17702642761827008341" style="display:none">Agatha Christie never runs out on ideas, now, does she? And this is her 31st installment of the Poirot series, among others. <br /><br /><i> After the Funeral </i> is another great murder-mystery story by her. It is a very interesting story with a strong, intelligent, and original plot. I was quite taken in for a ride with so many turns that I was a little disoriented as to the crime and the criminal. I assure you, the crime was baffling too because after all, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ one turned out to be a hoax!  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. I had a certain idea of the true criminal, but the motive and ingenuity in the execution of the crime, I would never have guessed. It would have been impossible. <br /><br />The solving of the crime was entirely based on analyzing the human mind and character by the close observation of the words and conduct of the suspected characters. This relying more on human psychology than on physical evidence (which I personally enjoy) is more and more common in these later installments. <br /><br />There are more subtle changes that this series has slowly undergone. One is the changes that have taken place over the years in British society - the generation clashes and the modern thinking and daring living of the young generation. The other is how Poirot has aged. The great detective is still in his prime mind, but he cannot evade the slow physical changes that are coming over him. These carefully thought inclusions make this series live and very real. In addition to the interest generated by the mystery story, these subtleties have added to my enjoyment of Poirot mysteries. <br /><br />Overall, it was a great read. And this one is definitely one of the best in the series.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17702642761827008341" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_156972821" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/156972821" />
    <a title="Andy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1251066-andy"><img alt="Andy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593516272p2/1251066.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/156972821?book_show_action=true">Mar 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andy" href="/user/show/1251066-andy">Andy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780007280605" href="/book/show/7819797-after-the-funeral">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1251066-andy?shelf=poirot">poirot</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1251066-andy?shelf=2011">2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer156972821" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3786084902873379583">Ah Poirot, how I enjoy you with your funny egg shaped head, your sleek moustaches and your willingness to embrace the rampant racism present in the average Englishman all in the pursuit of a tricksy murderer. This lovely hardcover facsimile edition has been sitting on my shelf for a while waiting to be read and it's really a very enjoyable Poirot whodunnit.<br/><br/>We have a stately home reaching the end of it's life along with the owner, a death swiftly followed by a second and a small cast of eccentric</span>
  <span id="freeText3786084902873379583" style="display:none">Ah Poirot, how I enjoy you with your funny egg shaped head, your sleek moustaches and your willingness to embrace the rampant racism present in the average Englishman all in the pursuit of a tricksy murderer. This lovely hardcover facsimile edition has been sitting on my shelf for a while waiting to be read and it's really a very enjoyable Poirot whodunnit.<br /><br />We have a stately home reaching the end of it's life along with the owner, a death swiftly followed by a second and a small cast of eccentric and slightly stereotypical family members. Underlying it is a will, a reasonable amount of money and a statement that murder may have been committed. For in this one we're not even sure if there has been.<br /><br />All very typical Christie and Poirot. Except this book seems more vested in the era than some of the others. Post war Britain, rationing, the fall of an empire, taxation issues and a changing cultural background plant the novel historically and it's richer for it. I found it took a few chapters to really get into but then it sucks you in. Poirot doesn't show up for a while and even when he does he's often in the background, quietly setting people off and observing the outcome. As such we don't get as many of those wonderful character moments he's known for. Still, this one had me guessing and the final resolution was surprising and unexpected, even though a fairly big clue is given to us earlier (for those really paying attention - you'll kick yourself when it's revealed - to date this makes it Agatha 12 : Andy 1).<br /><br />I really enjoyed this one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3786084902873379583" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer213203377" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7672848891396614046">The story starts after the funeral of Richard Abernethie, when his scatterbrain sister Cora makes a wild statement: "But he was murdered, wasn't he?" There is no such scandal, and everybody admonishes Cora - but the seed of doubt has been sown. Because the silly Cora has in the past been known to blurt out unwelcome truths.<br/><br/>Then, the next day, Cora is bludgeoned to death...<br/><br/>-------------------------------------------<br/><br/>This is an exquisitely structured novel with plenty of characterisation. As with a</span>
  <span id="freeText7672848891396614046" style="display:none">The story starts after the funeral of Richard Abernethie, when his scatterbrain sister Cora makes a wild statement: "But he was murdered, wasn't he?" There is no such scandal, and everybody admonishes Cora - but the seed of doubt has been sown. Because the silly Cora has in the past been known to blurt out unwelcome truths.<br /><br />Then, the next day, Cora is bludgeoned to death...<br /><br />-------------------------------------------<br /><br />This is an exquisitely structured novel with plenty of characterisation. As with all of Christie's novels, it uses the classic technique of misdirection, but it would have to be a very astute reader who can spot it. I suggest all Christie fans to reread the novel and marvel at her craft.<br /><br />Five stars, without a doubt.</span>

          </span>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2374163473?book_show_action=true">Feb 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="NILTON TEIXEIRA" class="user" itemprop="url" name="NILTON TEIXEIRA" href="/user/show/15546696-nilton-teixeira">NILTON TEIXEIRA</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6251567-after-the-funeral">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2374163473" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7753456835722638741">I was introduced to Agatha Christie during my teens, by my older sister who was an avid reader.<br/>I’m a huge fan and she’s been my favourite author. I have read all of her works (including the plays and the romance series). She is probably the only author that I don’t mind re-reading.<br/>This is the third time that I read this book, but the first time in English.<br/>In my opinion this is not one of her greatest work.<br/>Don’t get me wrong. It still has all the elements that made her the queen of mystery.<br/>Once a</span>
  <span id="freeText7753456835722638741" style="display:none">I was introduced to Agatha Christie during my teens, by my older sister who was an avid reader.<br />I’m a huge fan and she’s been my favourite author. I have read all of her works (including the plays and the romance series). She is probably the only author that I don’t mind re-reading.<br />This is the third time that I read this book, but the first time in English.<br />In my opinion this is not one of her greatest work.<br />Don’t get me wrong. It still has all the elements that made her the queen of mystery.<br />Once again she introduces us to a cast of characters who have all the reasons to commit a murder. And none of the characters are likeable.<br />But during the first 28%, before Hercule Poirot gets involved, I thought that the language and the dialogues were very stiff and a bit boring.<br />Fortunately everything changes, thanks to the incredible and unbelievable Monsieur Poirot.<br />The ending, although plausible and satisfying, felt rushed.<br />If you have never read Agatha Christie, I would not recommend this one as a starter, but it is still a good read, just not as exciting as the other books.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7753456835722638741" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4472761640139764962"><i>“It is a profound belief of mine that if you can induce a person to talk to you for long enough, on any subject whatever, sooner or later they will give themselves away!” </i><br/><br/>Our dear Agatha does it again, offering us a really vile group of people, all perfectly possible murderers, and yet baffling you once more. I did remember who it was but this allowed me to focus on the rest instead and lead me to wonder if sometimes the crime is only the carrot she dangles to attract her readers, the ‘meat’ of </span>
  <span id="freeText4472761640139764962" style="display:none"><i>“It is a profound belief of mine that if you can induce a person to talk to you for long enough, on any subject whatever, sooner or later they will give themselves away!” </i><br /><br />Our dear Agatha does it again, offering us a really vile group of people, all perfectly possible murderers, and yet baffling you once more. I did remember who it was but this allowed me to focus on the rest instead and lead me to wonder if sometimes the crime is only the carrot she dangles to attract her readers, the ‘meat’ of her novels being instead in the studies in psychology and sociology she acts, all with a good dose of humour and irony. Her characters are so recognisable as ‘personalities' seen in the real world that I can only imagine too well our author surrepticiously, or not, taking notes of everything she saw around her. </span>

A remarkable writer and the best of her genre, for me.</span>

<div id="review_2076600854" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2076600854" />
    <a title="BrokenTune" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10592271-brokentune"><img alt="BrokenTune" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1391017756p2/10592271.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2076600854?book_show_action=true">Aug 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="BrokenTune" class="user" itemprop="url" name="BrokenTune" href="/user/show/10592271-brokentune">BrokenTune</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007562695" href="/book/show/25695146-after-the-funeral">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10592271-brokentune?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2076600854" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14881779386784369319">3.5*<br/><br/><i>Should murder make sense? Mr Entwhistle wondered. Academically the answer was yes. But many pointless crimes were on record. It depended, Mr Entwhistle reflected, on the mentality of the murderer.</i><br/><br/>This was my first time reading After the Funeral, but, of course, it is the story that is loosely used as the story in Murder at the Gallop, that classic Marple film starring the incomparable Margaret Rutherford. <br/> <br/>This is all wrong, of course. After the Funeral is a Poirot mystery, not a Marple. <br/> <br/>Bu</span>
  <span id="freeText14881779386784369319" style="display:none">3.5*<br /><br /><i>Should murder make sense? Mr Entwhistle wondered. Academically the answer was yes. But many pointless crimes were on record. It depended, Mr Entwhistle reflected, on the mentality of the murderer.</i><br /><br />This was my first time reading After the Funeral, but, of course, it is the story that is loosely used as the story in Murder at the Gallop, that classic Marple film starring the incomparable Margaret Rutherford. <br /> <br />This is all wrong, of course. After the Funeral is a Poirot mystery, not a Marple. <br /> <br />But other than this, the main plot is the same: a family gathering, a vague accusation, a brutal murder. Poirot joins the plot relatively late, and then potters about as Poirot does.<br /> <br />Which brings me to following: Poirot is the worst person to ever try and disguise himself. This is not the first time he does it, and every time I've seen him do it, it is just ridiculous.<br />Surely, Christie wrote this "disguise" malarkey as a bit of fun with the oh so famous and infallible Belgian.<br /><i><br />And his name isn’t Pontarlier – it’s Hercules something.’ ‘Hercule Poirot – at your service.’ Poirot bowed. There were no gasps of astonishment or of apprehension. His name seemed to mean nothing at all to them. They were less alarmed by it than they had been by the single word ‘detective’.</i><br /><br />After the Funeral is a fairly standard Poirot story, not the best, not the worst. There are a couple of things that do not work, like Poirot's attempt at making people confess to him. That was just plain silly.<br /> <br />However, I liked disliking most of the characters. It's a bit of dark satire, more than a murder mystery, but it isn't as good as other Poirot stories.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14881779386784369319" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1997960708" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1997960708" />
    <a title="Jim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36199787-jim"><img alt="Jim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1445213837p2/36199787.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1997960708?book_show_action=true">May 12, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/36199787-jim">Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6251567-after-the-funeral">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36199787-jim?shelf=books-read-2018">books-read-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36199787-jim?shelf=mystery-suspense-thriller">mystery-suspense-thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1997960708" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9021210996770234058"><i>"...reading an Agatha Christie novel is, above all else, great fun." - <br/>Sophie Hannah</i><br/><br/>Open an <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/123715.Agatha_Christie' title='Agatha Christie' rel='nofollow noopener'>Agatha Christie</a> book and you step into a different time and a different world. Here the time is post World War II but the setting feels Victorian. There is the mansion, the old and loyal butler, and of course there is the family.<br/><br/>The story opens with the family of the late Richard Abernethie returning to his mansion following the funeral. Abernethie's sister, Cora Lansquenet, shocks everyone when she sudde</span>
  <span id="freeText9021210996770234058" style="display:none"><i>"...reading an Agatha Christie novel is, above all else, great fun." - <br />Sophie Hannah</i><br /><br />Open an <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/123715.Agatha_Christie" title="Agatha Christie" rel="nofollow noopener">Agatha Christie</a> book and you step into a different time and a different world. Here the time is post World War II but the setting feels Victorian. There is the mansion, the old and loyal butler, and of course there is the family.<br /><br />The story opens with the family of the late Richard Abernethie returning to his mansion following the funeral. Abernethie's sister, Cora Lansquenet, shocks everyone when she suddenly says <i>“He was murdered, wasn’t he?”</i> The others, after their initial shock and surprise, put it off as Cora being Cora. She was a bit of a scatterbrain and tended to just blurt things out. But the very next day Cora Lansquenet is savagely murdered. No question about the cause of her death. A hatchet. And the family lawyer begins to wonder if it is related to what she said after the funeral. To find the answer he calls his friend, Hercule Poirot.<br /><br />Poirot is now elderly and retired. At times it seemed as though Dame Christie must be getting tired of her most famous character. He does not even make an appearance until chapter 7. Often the story is told through other characters such as the family lawyer. Poirot is disappointed at one point to learn that no one recognizes his name. And when Poirot says <i>“The time has come to tell you all.”</i> Inspector Morton responds <i>“Yes, yes, tell me all! And for the Lord’s sake hurry up and do it!”</i><br /><br />Poirot has always used his little grey cells, employing logic and talking to people, through what he calls <i>“the dangers of conversation”</i>. As he says, <i>“if you can induce a person to talk to you for long enough, on any subject whatever, sooner or later they will give themselves away</i>. Where this story shines is in the resolution where once again Christie's ingenuity is apparent. Overall this was a 3 star book but with the ending perhaps 3.5 stars. Not one of my favorites but still a fun read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9021210996770234058" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2620655041" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2620655041" />
    <a title="Elizabeth (Alaska)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2026178-elizabeth-alaska"><img alt="Elizabeth (Alaska)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1304966095p2/2026178.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2620655041?book_show_action=true">May 27, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth (Alaska)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth (Alaska)" href="/user/show/2026178-elizabeth-alaska">Elizabeth (Alaska)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6251567-after-the-funeral">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=author-christie-poirot">author-christie-poirot</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=mid-20th-century">mid-20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2026178-elizabeth-alaska?shelf=women">women</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2620655041" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1178869550957051274">I think Christie is the best after a long hard day or after a hard read. Unfortunately, I wasn't experiencing either of these conditions when I picked this up. Her prose is fairly simple and she offers little in the way of characterization. Christie is for plot. OK, it's for plot, just go with it, I told myself.<br/><br/>There is the premise here that a man who for all appearances has died a natural death, may have been poisoned. Poirot is consulted. Without giving too much away, it is easy to see the pre</span>
  <span id="freeText1178869550957051274" style="display:none">I think Christie is the best after a long hard day or after a hard read. Unfortunately, I wasn't experiencing either of these conditions when I picked this up. Her prose is fairly simple and she offers little in the way of characterization. Christie is for plot. OK, it's for plot, just go with it, I told myself.<br /><br />There is the premise here that a man who for all appearances has died a natural death, may have been poisoned. Poirot is consulted. Without giving too much away, it is easy to see the premise is plausible and that Poirot will discover the culprit. There were surely enough people who had both motive and opportunity.<br /><br />It isn't that I don't enjoy the few hours I spend on any Christie novel. I do. However, any 4-stars will be few and far between, and this is just a happy three.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1178869550957051274" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1422740184" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1422740184" />
    <a title="Obsidian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35942740-obsidian"><img alt="Obsidian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1509845837p2/35942740.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1422740184?book_show_action=true">Oct 22, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Obsidian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Obsidian" href="/user/show/35942740-obsidian">Obsidian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780062073822" href="/book/show/10033363-after-the-funeral">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1422740184" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12854232616009540431">Only read this book if you want to read all of the Poirot books. Otherwise skip it. Seriously.<br/><br/>Some of the most despicable characters and an ending that had to be the dumbest one yet for a Christie novel.<br/><br/>Once again we have very little Poirot to start with. This book starts at the reading of the will of the late Richard Abernethie. The whole family is left money, though his sister Cora mentions that he had to have been murdered. This disturbs other members of the family and the family solicitor, M</span>
  <span id="freeText12854232616009540431" style="display:none">Only read this book if you want to read all of the Poirot books. Otherwise skip it. Seriously.<br /><br />Some of the most despicable characters and an ending that had to be the dumbest one yet for a Christie novel.<br /><br />Once again we have very little Poirot to start with. This book starts at the reading of the will of the late Richard Abernethie. The whole family is left money, though his sister Cora mentions that he had to have been murdered. This disturbs other members of the family and the family solicitor, Mr. Entwhistle. Mr. Entwhistle is disturbed because Cora has a knack for noticing things that other members of her family ignores and after she is murdered by someone he feels that Cora was correct and Mr. Abernethie was murdered. <br /><br />I refuse to discuss any of these people because they were all as shallow as a small puddle formed after a brief rain. Once again I question how Agatha Christie viewed women. Based on her life I always imagined her as independent and sure of herself. She always seems to write women in these Poirot books as either clinging to a man that does not want them or dealing with a man who treats them terribly by cheating and or beating them but they still want to be with.<br /><br />The flow was really bad. It took eons (it felt like) for the story to get going. We have Poirot going in and stirring up the Abernethie relations and all you find out is more information to either make you loathe or pity these people depending on who the story focuses on.<br /><br />The setting of post-war England in this one was much more explicit than in the previous two novels. Unlike in prior books, if a wealthy man/woman died most of the servants could be taken care of after the fact, however, in this book it is specifically mentioned because of taxes and how high everything costs to date that a lot of household items must be sold along with homes. I think even Poirot and others remark a time or two that they don't seem to like the new world in which England has entered where there is less of a master/servant relationship among people. In fact I think the ending was in a way taking a look at what the war had wrought in terms of good versus bad servants. <br /><br />The ending was a joke. I mean it was just the dumbest thing ever. I re-read it twice to make sure that I was not mis-reading anything, but nope that was the ending. <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br />We find out that Cora's companion murdered her all to sell a Vermeer painting she had in order to get money to open up a tea shop. It makes no sense for this woman to have pretended to be Cora, let alone to say that perhaps Richard Abernethie was murdered. Why didn't she just kill Cora and abscond with the painting? It made no sense at all. I honestly think that this was Christie's way of saying that before the war a woman like Miss Gilchrist would have been happy with her lot in life, being a paid companion to a now wealthy woman. Instead she's angry and frustrated that she can't do a lifelong dream of opening her own tea shop. <br /> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />And then we have all parties just go off and someone just exist unhappily together.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12854232616009540431" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('y1q8kMEXdXvumMDsoy/LNdetOxswa3wPMWTDf6aF3bWXHmGdd3nuJb87VLVeRrCn7tU52+S1E6Xf9gdpZ7vSOQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('TgIIaEQHe/w3rly7oQ9HSVBGHisViiToezOFyVMAuEESRtVl8mngomYNyOJcZjzbaT4c68FUS0KVoUHfkj63zQ==')})
  }
